The â€œmicro/macroâ€ problem is different.  0.Misunderstandings come from a methodological oversight. 1. The probabilistic interpretation of physical phenomena by quantum theory implies the symmetry of the results: 50/50. 2. However, such an interpretation by quantum theory is a priori extrapolated to asymmetric phenomena.  3. For example, in the case of Schrodinger's cat, the radiation source is considered as a symmetrical/probabilistic (50/50) element of the experiment, however, it is combined with an asymmetric participant: a cat that, being alive or dead, has asymmetric states. 4. For clarity, you can â€œslightlyâ€ modify the experiment: place a dead cat in a "black box" with a radiation source. It seems that now even the most faithful follower of quantum theory will not claim that the cat is both dead and alive at the same time or will come to life in another universe. {The cunning plan â€œBâ€ with a sleeping cat is: â€œSweeping garbage under the carpet.â€ (Feynman) and is addressed to inattentive (sleepy) followers of quantum theory.} 5. It is better to put illuminated photo paper in a drawer: it is unlikely that the paper will react to the actions of the 50/50 source in such a way as to become usable again.  6. Events and phenomena themselves exist independently of the frame of reference, but their specific manifestations and quantitative ratios in different frames of reference (attention!) they can be completely different.* 7. So, if in your frame of reference you observe / measure and/ or evaluate / identify the probabilistic nature of a certain physical process in another frame of reference, for example, for a coin: 50/50; then this is your right, since all frames of reference are equal (not to be confused with equivalence). {By the way, entangled particles: each of the particles in its own frame of reference is equal with its spin, for example, up, with another particle with spin down, in a different coordinate system, but is not equivalent to the other.} 8. And in the system associated with the tossed coin, there is no probability: the usual natural physical process takes place here, and there is no need to move to another universe.** 9. It is clear that with all possible interpretations of quantum theory, the "tradition of one-sidednessâ€, unfortunately, stupidly/intrusively holds: for now. Even in the multi-world and with the â€œappearance of properties in particles due to measurement", Bell, Wignerâ€¦ Hence the â€œobserver effect".  10.The uncertainty principle is the result of a misunderstanding of what probability/equivalence is. In the Heisenberg inequalities, the mathematical apparatus was formed before the interpretation of their physical essence. It is funny that these inequalities indicate the absence of exact values of coordinates and momentum vector in the states of microobjects at the same time; and thus exclude the equivalence of these parameters. 11.Finally, the evaluation of the state function of the system under study (the universe) depends on the choice of the reference frame and coordinate system: the result of the measurement is a change in the state of the measuring device, a change in the physical /biological state of the observer, a change in the mental / intellectual state of the observer, that is, the detection of the next phase of evolution its own frame of reference; - thanks to which measurement becomes possible. 12. Jokes with time asymmetry are inexcusable even for such a "blindly lucky quantum theory". ---------------- *) - The equations that formulate the laws of physics should be invariant with respect to any coordinate transformations: "Equality" and "the same flow of processes" are not analogous concepts: and the word "relativity" itself contributed to the confusion of the concepts of "relative motion" and "relativism of phenomena".  The relativity of motion determines the immutability of laws: the ambiguity of the word "relativity", when the content changes depending on whether it is applied to motion or to the formulation of physical laws, caused many errors." (Tonnela, Updating the concept of relativity in Einstein's physics). **) - When Einstein inadvertently noted: "God does not play dice," Bohr rightly pointed out: "One should not tell God how to rule the world."  However, Einstein apparently meant that there is no randomness for bones in a lonely and aging Universe.

We can only experience what happens in our mind. If there is something  outside our mind, we can only experience/be aware of it thru the effects (direct or very indirect) it can have in our mind. The assumption that there exist a "reality" outside our mind is just a useful assumption.  Useful to "predict" and being able to exert certain influence over our own vital experience (for instance to predict that if you walk inside a big fire, you will have an undesirable vital experience).  We can only know something about reality thru a mental representation of that "reality". We cannot know how that reality "really is", beyond our mental representation. A good mental representation is the one that is more useful for us to predict and then act to avoid undesirable vital experiences or to cause desirable vital experiences.
What do you mean by cosmological event horizon? Earth has event horizon about a billion to one, the galaxy has event horizon about a light year, one to 100,000, but the function is constant R (in kilometers) = 3M in solar masses. The observable Universe has a radius of 3 billion light years a ratio of one to ten, so the answer is the yes, the event horizon of a critical mass singularity is bigger than the total Universe. The expansion never reaches the event horizon.

The foundation of the universe is very simply â€œstructureâ€. Different properties emerge from different structures.   Start with the smallest thing, nothing, and change its structure - start with space itself and vibrate it in different ways - now you have different things, different strings, and those each have different properties. Start combining them together and you get more different things, quarks and protons and neutrons and electrons. Change their structure - add more electrons - and you get different properties- atoms of different elements. Keep combining different things and you keep getting different structures with different properties. Now youâ€™ve got different elements, different molecules, different systems. Keep going and structures get more complicated, these structures have new emergent properties. Youâ€™ll end up with something as complicated as the human brain, which has the emergent property of consciousness based on sensory input and information processing, which can all be boiled down to the way that the brain functions based on its structure.  There is only structure. Different structures result in different properties. Thatâ€™s it. Realism, the unverifiable idea of an ever unobservable, unimaginable world fundamentally different from and independent of the only thing we can ever recognize as real; consciousness (in its pure form and in its modulated form; as perceptions, thoughts and sensations) is very different from intersubjectivity, the recognition of the inherent subjectivity of all (experience) and meaningful relations between individual minds that can be conceptualized by a world interacting with all those minds without being essentially different from them. The "20 questions" example in the intro perfectly illustrated something I've been racking my brain trying to articulate: the phenomenon of observation/measurement is generative, and always yields a "consistent history." When an observer in one frame becomes entangled with a system through observation, their frame's wavefunction collapses into a state consistent with all already-observed information. When an observer in a second frame meets the first one to compare notes, they become entangled with the first and their frame collapses into a state that preserves a consistent history with the frame of the first. We're effectively inhabiting separate spacetime worms which are constantly becoming entangled to create "objective reality," and the preservation of a consistent history by wavefunction collapse is what yields shared facts, laws of the universe, etc.

I think we need to ban the term observer from the vernacular of theoretical physics. Two particles interacting act as a measuring system, however given what we know about QM and GR, whose to say which particle is the "observer" and which is the "observed" ? The two particles have degrees of freedom that are wave functions related to the forces of the SM, however when the interact and the wave functions collapse, there is a symmetry that prohibits a special frame as to which particle played what role. From the perspective of either particle they would be considered preferentially the "observer"but that cannot be so if there is no universal context for that moniker, just at there is no universal clock in GR. A photon that left a quasar in a galaxy shortly after the BB and traveled split pathed by a massive body like a black hole, could be "observed" by anything, a retina, a molecule in space or an atmosphere, whatever. However that delayed collapse and choice 13 billion years post ex facto doesn't necessarily make the interacting particle now the observer. Time ought not dictate causality in that way right?

Great series Matt! How can there be an observer, an observed, and the relationship between them, without them in some sense already being real? At the level of mathematics, we can invent/describe any 'physical' entity and interaction, and while we are manipulating the logical symbols of theory, reality is imbued with infinite degrees of potential magic, but for those which happen to correspond with what is 'out there' the magic becomes transformed into a concrete description of a real (or 'more real') reality. The point is: there is nothing inherent in the mathematical symbols which hints at this 'reality producing' aspect of physics; that is established only by experimentation and a lot of empirical curve fitting etc. Don't know if I'm getting my point across - maybe it's to say that mathematical meaning and physical meaning are two completely different things, and we may be confusing them in QM.

I understand that any particle is the event created by a collapse of quantum wave. This collapse is a result of the interaction between two or more quantum waves, for example the wave of a photon and the wave of a particle in a detector. After the collapse, a new quantum wave is created at the location of the event. This means that we create the reality by an act of observation which also creates a new yet unobserved state of the reality.

Quantum Mechanics & Quantum Field Theory traffic in *what we know* about a system rather than what is going on inside that system. Probabilities of scenarios occurring until we actually witness the outcome. Collapse occurring at the moment of observation because the energy needed to observe these events is so comparable to the energy of the events themselves that it actually disturbs them.  Itâ€™s pretty obvious that the cat inside the box is â€œboth alive and deadâ€ until we actually observe the outcome of the random event: itâ€™s like trying to predict a coin-toss ahead of the event. So, in the game Reverse 20 questions, suppose that player 1 (the person who answers the questions) assumes the rule that they will answer 'yes' or 'no' to the questions alternatively, regardless of what the questions are. Then suppose that player 2 (the person asking questions) asks the following question: "Is it larger than a basketball?" Player 1 answers "Yes". Players 2 asks, "Can its shape and size change?" Player 1 answers "No". Suppose that player 2 then asks, "Is it smaller than a baseball?" If player 1 answers "yes", then there is a contradiction between the first and third question. How can something be both larger than a basketball and smaller than a baseball, especially because it cannot change its shape and size?  So, my question, to whoever wishes to explore it, is basically this: what happens in the game reverse 20 questions if it a produces a contradiction such as the one above, and how does this apply in the context of quantum mechanics?  Edit: I'm aware that, realistically, player 2 wouldn't ask the third question knowing the information derived from the first, but I'm just curious how this would pan out in the context of quantum mechanics.
One interesting thing I'd like to note is that this seems to map precisely onto a similar question in mathematics: Are we creating new mathematics, or are we discovering already existing maths?   The answer seems to be roughly similar, too.

This idea makes perfect sense to me. It seems like the same type of situation as general relativity. Without two different frames of reference interacting with each other, there can't be many judgements about what the "facts" of a given situation are. Am I stationary or moving at a constant velocity? Etc. Its all a little fuzzy and basically kind of meaningless unless there's another situation you can use for comparison. "Relative to what" is where the real action is. The fact that you can compare the two is what allows you to establish facts about either situation.  The "observer" thing is often misinterpreted, but I do think that its very reasonable to suggest that reality is relational, not objective. The facts about the world are established as a consquence of interactions between things, not by the things themselves. You might even say that there's no such things as "things themselves", its just relationships between things all the way down.

I think actually "materialism and empirico-criticism" is a very relevant text here as emphasizing the primacy of observation is effectively the same as the idea that the ultimate reality is just complexes of sensations. Our sensations are of course what we can verify is real but if these complexes of sensations have no objective external origin if there's no physical cause for why we're measuring the information that we're measuring then it ceases to be coherent why we're measuring anything at all. If there's not actually a tea pot in front of me why do see one? If there was no tea pot I wouldn't be seeing one although even if there is I may still not see it without that necessarily implying its not present. Its hard to prove a negative since theoretically you could be missing some piece of information youre forced to reckon with the teapot as a possibility. If there is a teapot in some physical volume why am I able to wave my hand through that space as if nothings there? The failure to find evidence of its existence does seem to confirm its absence but maybe you could object that owing to some special properties of its composition it doesn't interact with normal matter repulsively for example you could posit that the tea pot was actually made of dark matter even though certainly you could conclude that a perfectly "normal" tea pot didn't exist in that space. If nothing has any external seperate existence from our sensory perception of it and the only reality is pure sensations then they have no underlying causal nucleus in any objective rules the information is pure randomness yet we observe somewhat the opposite sure we find chaos but also from that we see order emerges and the two seem to spiral around one another in interplay with a guided rhythm. Measured information itself is arguably proof that an external objective reality must exist apart or else what would we measure if the big bang wasn't real (how do we define this?) but what the best models are to describe what the exact nature of this material reality is and whether our subjective measuring determines the information content of that reality even after the fact of its prior moments is an open question because even if the answers of our questions are predetermined (I dont believe thats necessarily true) and have a fully independent "real" existence before we ever even ask them we only arrive at those answers we always would have that are preconditions for our and still only attain them by means of observation which is our only means of interacting with the real. But either way it's imperative we comprehend as materialists that our sensations and measurements are not an impenetrable barrier seperating us from what externally objectively exists but rather a link connecting us to it.
If the big bang hadn't happened then there obviously wouldn't be satellite telescopes now to peer back in time and confirm that yes the big bang was a real event we will only ever verify a past which conforms with the prerequisites for the present. If your parents had never lived their lives before you were around till they met up and eventually created you then you'd never have been born to measure that the reality of your lives exists now and so their lives must've existed then independently of your interaction with it. If we were ever to do the opposite and make observations about the past which contradict our information about the present as we know it then we would be negating our reality and the preconditions for our existence if we asked if the earth existed a hundred years ago and found that no it didn't then obviously we couldn't be around now to arrive at that conclusion that our existence was a lie. To actually make that observation based off some empirical measurement we acquired by some means we're extremely confident of and got peer verified would be to radically alter our conception of reality to reconcile two known facts which appear to negate one another. In fact this is what has happened it is the history of our knowledge in the process of learning about the mechanics of reality through physics and natural philosophy long ago we constructed narratives about the nature of existence various models of reality by asking questions and arriving at answers until new experiments and discoveries disproved earlier conceptions negating their validity and mandating we conjure up alternative explanations to reconcile with our new observations.

A person must build a house, plant garden. An unattended house will dilapidate gradually on its own, and a garden grows out quickly; but nothing builds itself into existence. The universe can't create itself. That would be the OPPOSITE of everything that all of us observe daily in life, that not even the extremely simple machinery of a very simple mousetrap can come together unassisted to catch the mouse. That's what the entropy effect dictates. Not only has the universe been created by Someone immensely powerful and wise, but he is evidently very actively maintaining the universe, as a gardener maintains a garden, to prevent it from the decay of the entropy effect.

That seems like circular logic so I don't think I entirely understand the premise.  I guess what I'm trying to figure out is: whether the universe's future determined it's past qualities, the superposition lasted until there was some "observer"  (but was unable to be defined before the first "observation"), or if there was simply no way to create the universe (with us inside) except in the exact configuration we have now. When I try to imagine a superposition of the universe's states, I almost envision it to be a blurry mess all the way down to the smallest scale. Except, in this case, your glasses/contacts might be the old ones that didn't work so you have to keep looking for the right ones.  Maybe that doesn't make sense or is way off base. I just feel like there's something quite intuitive about the weak anthropic principle if we are trying to going to accept the randomness of the outcomes/properties of quantum objects.

I find this video disturbing in terms of the content and the capability by those purported to be so brilliant, to wander from commons sense. I do very much enjoy this presenter, however.  Let me pose something here. Take the Schroedinger cat box and change it just a little.......inside we have the cat, the emitter, the receiver, the hammer and the vile, just like in the original. But in the vile we have acid instead of poison. The acid fumes would kill the cat as readily as the poison so that presents no problem. The vile would then be mounted on a mechanism such that were the cat to fall over, the vile would tip and its contents be dumped on the box floor. Suddenly, all those in witness would see acid dripping out from the bottom. The box would never opened and yet we would all know that by definition, the cat was dead and only dead for if not, no acid would be dripping out.  The only difference between the Schroedinger scenario and mine for the purposes of making my point is that the acid would eat through the box and the poison, not. In order for the â€œinformationâ€ as you call it to exist outside of the box at all, the string of deterministic events within the box would have to have taken place already, i.e., prior to the revelation of the dripping acid. The only way the acid could be dripping out is if the cat were dead to activate the mechanism which overturned the vile. That the dripping acid was visible but more importantly, that it in a sense, â€œwould seek outâ€ the observer to present itself (proactively â€“ it exists externally before the observer is made aware of it) means that the cat must be dead and only dead at that time. This is no different than the necessity of an electron gun to have already shot the photon at the slitted plate in the double slit experiment before it can create the pattern, interference or otherwise. The effect (the formation of some pattern), cannot precede the cause (the shot of the photon from the gun). You cannot claim that the acid would both be dripping and not dripping out of the box, the cat being both alive and standing (so the vile would not tip over) or dead (and having activated the mechanism and turned the vile over). In the case of the conventional understanding of the superposition of the cat, the cat exists in both states as a material entity imposing its form and function on empty space, which all entities do. Could you claim that the acid would drip from the box and not drip from the box at the same time so it would exist also in two different places at the same time, i.e., in the vile and not in the vile? But if the acid left the box and alarmed the observer â€œin its own timeâ€, how could you say it is not deterministic? Keep in mind, there was no control exerted by the observer in his going to open the box to see the state of the cat. In this case the observer was reactive only unlike in the Schroedinger version.  So where is the wave form that would collapse in this scenario? Where is the superposition?  As for your delayed choice experiment, why is it that you claim detector 1 would register all the particles and not detector 2 (after adding the second beam splitter) when 1 would be no different than 2? In either scenario, a mirror would reflect the particle after leaving the first beam splitter (I know itâ€™s one particle taking both paths at once) to the second beam splitter where they would cancel or combine and move to a detector. Why to detector 1 and not 2 when the function would be identical? In either case the cancelation or combination would be made by one moving perpendicular to the other. Additionally, why would it not continue to be a random reception by both detectors 1 and 2? Something is missing.   I think you should clarify what you mean by â€œinformationâ€. Information in the conventional understanding is not a thing or a substance. One cannot view an object and see information. Information is a consequence of a consciousness understanding an object or process and is realized only after that object exists, imposes its existence upon materiality and is studied that an understanding of it might be formulated. Yes objects impose their form and function on materiality as is made clear by the following;  As taught by physicists, materiality/empty space is a material construct. We can only understand this to be so because we have been instructed that particles are in fact vibrating energy quanta, local phenomena, peculiar to specific energy fields which in concert, form empty space. Empty space can be distorted or warped by the presence of a mass, each example of which is a composite of these energy quanta. I donâ€™t think we could conclude anything else.  I donâ€™t care how mysterious quantum physics is, physicists state that there can be no existent if there can be no molecules, no molecules if no atoms, no atoms if no protons, no protons if no quarks and gluons, and no gluons and quarks if no energy fields of that kind, the last of which must exist in measurable quantity in prerequisite. These are objective, deterministic â€œphysicalitiesâ€ which define the architecture or structure of the means of being which are not in any way influenced in their existence by our awareness or our observations which are of consequence only â€œafter the factâ€.  A tree exists in the woods whether we are aware of it or not as is made very clear by the death of him so unfortunate as to have built his house so close to the woodâ€™s edge that it in its collapse, it reached out to strike his house and kill him and did so when no one was aware to observe it. It seems you infer that there is a physical character to information when there is not and cannot be. That two particles behave in a manner by entanglement which is complex and functional in some way is not a matter of some information as some phenomenon prior to the physicality and physical law which governs it. It is that which comes â€œafterâ€ the existence and assertion of the phenomenon which we as observers come to finally understand. It is wholly reactive, â€œnotâ€ proactive. Physical law is a fabric, threads which flow throughout the universe, throughout the context of the means of existence which simply â€œareâ€ and do not require definition but that we need to define them that we might understand and employ that understanding to our benefit. I spend a lot of my free time thinking of fringe-science concepts and I have had this growing notion over the years that the causal relationship between everything in the past, present and future was exactly the theory he's describing - the participatory universe. Reality/time/causality, in the big picture, is less of a straight line and more of a loop that everything influences. The fact that it the loop exists as a necessity to satisfy the consistency of all of the parts is something I strongly concur with and I agree that it's not a mystical power from the conscious mind. "The reality we observe is the only reality that is consistent with all questions ask of it by all observers"  A related concept I came across a few years ago and personally termed "the banana peel mechanism" (I'm not actually sure what it's called) acts in a similar way, but the opposite effect - reality PREVENTS a loop from being created. [Fyi, it's less about the banana peel and more about the mechanism] The concept goes something like this: Suppose you've created the first time machine and you have the plans in front of you to replicate it. It took the the greater part of your life to figure it all out and now what you want to do is bring the plans back to your younger self and get all that time back. So, you pack your bags up and start walking to your machine BUT - you guessed it - you slip on a banana peel, which spills a drink into some electronics and soon enough, an enormous fire burns everything to rubble. Reality REALLY doesn't like paradoxes, so it stopped you from changing the past so that the future remained consistent - and it could have happened a million different ways.  Somewhere in my reading and research through the years I came to a deduction on these "loops" I've described - if you're in a loop, you can't get out & if you're not in a loop, you can't create one. It comes with a quite complicated caveat - things could be *slightly* different in the loops, so long as a "certain" consistency is maintained - ultimately the paradoxes are avoided at all costs. This comes back to how reality "could have stopped you a million different ways" - perhaps instead of slipping on a banana, a stray neutrino caused a malfunction and you exploded... who knows. It's kind of like some predetermined, Final Destination-like stuff...  I find it super intriguing that these loops bring up the question of free-will: are we actually free to make choices or has everything been predetermined?

Wow, the participatory universe makes so much sense to me. This actually leads me to a very interesting idea: What if all possible universes do exist, but this universe is just the one in which I, (my conscious mind) exists? What if the only objective reality is a superposition of all possible states of the universe that could exist? In some versions of the many worlds hypothesis, the wave function collapses from the point of view of an observer, when the observer becomes entangled with the particle that is observed. The only observer that I know for sure to exist is myself ("I think, therefore I am"). So what if the superposition collapses from my point of view into the universe that eventually produced me. And everything I interact with collapses from the superposition and creates my reality. This seems like some egoistical, solipsistic point of view, but it also could mean that all possible minds do exist in their own universe, where the superposition collapsed to create them. This whole idea is sort of an anthropic principle, but instead of arguing based on the fact that humans exist, I argue based on the fact that I exist.
If this is right, wouldn't it mean that two observers could never exist in the same universe? And wouldn't that mean that we all literally exist in our own universe?

Have long felt this way about the universe. I wonder if the set of "questions" that can be asked by each physically real particle, A, is constrained by whatever interaction collapsed A to begin with. Say B is collapsed into a 4 by C asking "what is a number between 1 and 10". Now B can only ask "what is a number between 1 and 4", A answers by collapsing into a 2.   This is a ropey analogy suggesting that electrons and photons are electrons and photons, because whatever collapses then to begin with can only "ask" a very narrow range of "questions", i.e the probability space is very tightly constrained by prior interactions.  This may seems a little obvious, but then perhaps you can go far enough back to eventually define a singular interaction describing the big bang. In a pre-universe information space where all possible "questions" can be asked simultaneously, one of the "answers" will inevitably collapse into the universe we observe.  As an aside, is it possible for a complex enough computational engine, fed information about other previous interactions, be able to further constrain the questions it can ask, in order to increase the likelihood of a particular answer. I really don't like the Schrodinger's cat experiment, because we're talking about an irreversible event (the death of the cat). So if the event that judges the outcome has a change of death >0 for a period of determined time that is less than the period that the observer is waiting before taking a measurement, or even worse there's a period of changing states for the judging event, then the chance of finding the the cat in a dead state should be considered almost close to 100%... I feel different if the outcome is for example a direction or any non irreversible permanent state.

I never had a problem with the observer in quantum mechanics. A neighboring particle in any possible combination functions as an "observer" as it cannot interact with the superpositioned version of the other atom. It's wavefunction had to collapse in order to make an interaction possible.  A photon that is absorbed by an electron caused the electrons wavefunction to collapse, thus the photon becomes a functional observer. It is like any particle that is about to interact with another atom, proton, electron or whatever entitiy you can name, causes the respective wavefunction to collapse. An electron cannot convey energy if it is still in a superposition state that is uncertain about impulse, velocity or location. It needs to have a collapsed wavefunction, in other words the object hit by the electron needs "the answers to react properly". The wavefunction needs to collapse to have a certain energy, impulse, frequency, spin or whatever to make a given reaction happen. I think physicist had a tendency to get confused by the words "measurement" and "observer", every interaction causes a wavefunction to collapse, it forces "a decision" or "answer" as you put it in your episode here. No conscious keeping track of information is involved. A measurement or "act of observation" can be any type of plain old physical or chemical interaction between any type of entities. Obviously the reality around also proves this, the solidity of objects is based on the fact that a solid object is made up of a dense network of compounds or atoms that constantly demand "answers" from their neighbors, there is not much of superpositioned states going on for those atoms. This results in the world we see, not much quantum effects happening, because the wavefunction is basically in a collapsed state almost all the time. Only once we start to dissect the physical world, by observing single atoms and single photons, "observation" becomes something scarce and special. In reality the "act of observation" the "interrogation of reality" is going on constantly all the time between all the objects that cross each others path in spacetime.

So here's my take: you don't need a brain to observe the universe.  Say you're a photon going though space, minding your own business.  You pass by a black hole.  Your eyes are the sides the photo, and you observe that the side closer to the black hole is a little slower than the side away from the black hole.  And so you curve.  When you encounter another atom's electrons, you observe the increased friction between you and the atom you just hit, which may cause you to stop being a photon and start being an excited electron for a while, or you observe the friction abruptly dropping, signifying that you weren't absorbed and instead reflected off the atom.

If you do this thought experiment: you are floating ball of skin, you can only touch, but have no thoughts, no emotions, no memories and you are floating in a void in which you do not interact with anything. You do not experience darkness, you experience nothing. From your perspective you are in a state of non-existence. Until you meet an identical ball into which you crash. It is in this meeting that you both become conscious, not of yourself but of the other, because you finally have met a point of reference. It is in the meeting between the two beings that consciousness derives. Now, the weird thing is that this thought experiment basically describes wave-function collapse, two particles exist in a state of superposition or rather in a state of non-existence, it is in the meeting with each other that they get a point of reference and thus collapse into a state of certainty or rather existence. Thus consciousness and wave-function collapse have a very, very striking resemblance. Maybe we've thought of it all wrong, consciousness is not a cause of wave-function collapse, consciousness is caused by wave-function collapse. Consciousness is rather a point in spacetime being in a state of existence, the received information of the rest of reality collapses inwards into this state of existence, from uncertainty to certainty.  I like to think that consciousness is a literal property of the universe, a mechanism that turns non-existence into existence, in other words the collapse of the wave-function itself. However it is extremely important to realize that this kind of consciousness-property of wave-function collapse is different from the conscious experience of our human minds. Our human mind is the result of 3,5 billion years of evolution that utilizes this mechanism of wave-function collapse, in order for the organic system to increase it's chance of interaction with reality. Inadvertently it also increases the chances of the rest of the surrounding reality to be interacted with. If you think of Feynman-paths than this isn't so strange either, because a consciousness organism basically has a extremely high chance of interaction, thus seen from a evolutionary perspective a consciousness organism is the most likely outcome to evolve. Our biological experience of reality is the result of a unfathomably complex system of conscious interactions between particles that collectively make up the experience of our conscious reality. Anything outside of our space of interaction exists in a state of non-existence, and if we do not interact with reality than both we and reality have become non-existent.  However if this true, how come there are so many separate conscious interactions, and how come that a conscious being in the perspective of another conscious being can be in a state of superposition? The answer to that I think lies in the strange property of all consciousnesses to be completely identical at all times: consciousness is a single point of existence at the exact center of spacetime, both the future and the past are in a perpetual state of non-existence from this point of perspective. From the perspective of consciousness, the universe is moving through this point of existence. This description is identical to all conscious experiences. Of course our human minds might experience things differently, where you see green I see red etc, but this experience is not a property of consciousness (which is a single point of existence in spacetime caused by the interaction of particles). It might very well be that there is only one single point of existence within the entirety of reality. The entire lifetime of the universe, of spacetime, loops through this single point of existence, thus creating itself from nothing into everything. This would mean that their only exists one consciousness at the time in the universe, and that we all share the same cosmic consciousness throughout the entirety of spacetime.  Of course this is all philosophy, but I find it a beautiful interpretation to live by. Not only that, I can't think of a more empathetic perspective on reality, that we all might share the same consciousness, separated only by a strange timeloop.

In the denotational semantics of lazily evaluated functional programming languages we can represent algebraic data types as Scott domains, a kind of semilattice of partially defined values ordered by information content. Ever since I studied them I've wondered if they have a connection to the idea of the state of the universe being dependent on observation. In the participatory universe theory it's not only the set of all possible observations (ordered by precision of knowledge) that forms a Scott domain, but actually the set of all possible universes (ordered by degree of definedness). Whenever an observation is made, the universe becomes the supremum (least upper bound) of all universes that are consistent with the observations made so far. And the future light cone, in a sense, is the filter whose infimum (greatest lower bound) is the universe we're in at the current instant in time.

The aspects of this sort of quantum observer effect that most interest me are when the interactions occur over vast distances. Ten billion years ago, some galaxy near the edge of our own observable universe emitted a photon into deep space. This photon didn't just go in one direction, but according to QM 'rippled' outwards like a wave, potentially interacting with countless octillions of other particles along the way. The ripple expanded outwards in many directions, through nebulae, planets, other stars, probably other telescopes belonging to aliens *outside our observable universe* and by chance it didn't interact with any of them, passing over all of them without a blip - until one day that wave - now 20-billion light-years wide - suddenly resolved as a discrete ping on the CCD of the Hubble Telescope. The photon who's position was indeterminate for over 10-billion years, possibly anywhere within our observable universe or well outside it, suddenly was THERE for just a moment, and likewise there may have been an instantaneous entangled quantum interaction with the electron responsible for emitting that photon so long ago - even though the galaxy from which it came is probably now far beyond our particle horizon. Equally interesting, as photons move at the speed of light and thus do not experience time, that interaction was - from its standpoint, if it could be said to have one - instantaneous.
Kind of wish this would be more regularly couched by 'interaction' rather than observation. Despite the quantum 'fuzziness' of the existence of individual particles as they dash through the universe (or tabletop light beam experiments), most things WE experience have a much more significant and solid reality - because all the particles that make up us and our environment are interacting *constantly*, billions of times per second. This prevents any sort of 'quantum fuzzy woo' from cropping up in our lives, because all the stuff of us and our world is essentially pinned down by these interactions. Only when you get into rarefied experiments, or photons traveling endless light years through space does this sort of quantum observer/interaction weirdness generally become relevant. Honestly, Relativity already told us that interaction is critical to the underpinnings of reality - it's literally right there in the name - because you cannot define the properties of any particle EXCEPT as they relate to some other particle! That relationship and their interactions between each other are the actual important part. All forces and vectors are measurements between particles, not of particles. You can't even measure the mass of a particle in total isolation, because even mass is a relative value, as seen by the way particles can gain mass as they approach the speed of light.

I just solved quantum uncertainty while watching this video. It's so simple, I don't know why I didn't see it before. Whenever A interacts with B such that information of A transfers into B, all properties of A which B now possesses information about have been altered. So in a way, you can never observe what's really there, but only what was there until you observed it. But quantum information isn't the only way to obtain wisdom; you can still learn to predict what has actually happened by studying patterns and using analysis. The photon isn't in a superposition of going down both paths, it is a fundamentally different object. Interacting with the "pre-photon" in any way which collects pre-photon details will also cause it to become a "post-photon" which has different properties. But we can never see two kinds of photons because of the way observation fundamentally works.

None of them seem to provide a definitive answer to the question, does the universe exist without observers? I often come back to the first stanza of the Tao Te Ching which, to me, implies that the more we strive for this answer, the further we get from it. That it is ineffable. Whenever a proton, electron, or photon is shot out of a gun, it's respective magnetic field interacts with the magnetic fields of the electrons in the atoms and molecules of the gun itself, the medium the projectile is traveling through (ie: air), and/or from around the slits themselves. 5. The "delayed choice" experiment would only be puzzling if one didn't expect a polarizer to polarize light.  In other words, before passing the first polarizer, the photon could have been generated in any polarization, the first polarizer applies a torque that allows for light to choose between vertical or horizontal.  Once that is done, the horizontal photon will remain horizontal and the vertical photon will remain vertical.  There is no surprise or the need for self-interference to explain light always arriving onto a single detector. What was missing in the current explanation is that a polarizer is not a passive device. It will add torque to light.  The time-dependent Schrodinger's equation for a photon traveling in a vacuum would not change that photon polarization. In fact, polarization doesn't even enter Schrodinger's equation.  The only "mysterious" step in this experiment is "the observation performed by the first polarizer".  That is trivially explained by a torque.  The interaction of light with matter is modeled by the exchange of momentum (light interaction with a crystal, for example). It is no skin off my nose to understand that a torque could be applied by a polarizer.  Compare that hypothesis with the current one where a whole Universe is created at every single interaction of light with a polarizer!!!!!  Occam's Razor would force each and every one of those amazing scientists to just accept the simplest hypothesis or accept that their proposal is a barbaric dogma.

It sounds like the universe is lazily computing everything. Sometimes in computing you avoid performing an expensive operation until it's needed, at which point the required input is passed to the retrieval function and the actual result is provided back. Maybe the universe is doing the same thing. Furthermore, these lazy computations can be chained together. For example, I can know my value is the result of some expensive computation that results from inputting the results of many other lazy computations. No point in the network actually knows what it's value is, but if there is an "interaction" with one node in the network it could trigger a chain reaction that requires evaluating all the nodes. What's to say the network can't reference nodes in the past? There's no risk of any causal paradoxes because had that information been relevant for some prior causal purpose the node would have been evaluated already.

To think about the nature of the universe first you need to think about the nature of thinking. If consciousness is quantum (as proposed by Roger Penrose and Stuart Hameroff in their Orch-OR hypothesis) then any questions we ask which fail to acknowledge that fact are doomed to be unanswerable. If consciousness is not quantum, then the nature of the universe should be easily defined by classical means, and the answers should come from forces and circumstances that are well understood. The fact that we cannot determine the nature of the universe is the best circumstantial evidence I know of for the idea that consciousness is a non-classical, non-computational phenomenon.

All those interpretation wars fall prey to the same fallacy so common in positivistic science: if you know the tiniest bit you can extrapolate in an additive manner. Observing a single "particle" in the delayed choice experiment implies that the wave function is spread out all over the experimental setup. This one particle then, interacting with itself to construct 1 single measurement. That single measurement is real, but it does not constitute "reality". You can apply the cat analogy to just about every unknown random event.  The weather at the north pole is either sunny or cloudy. Using the cat analogy, you can say that the weather at the north pole is both sunny and cloudy. When the satellite that is over the north pole takes a picture you can report if it is sunny or cloudy. Same thing as reporting if the cat is dead or alive when you open the box. I am not a physicist but Feynman also sounds the most reasonable and rational to me.  Whether something happens as a result of a random event like a bolt of lightning striking a tree or as a result of some biological life like a bear deciding to catch and eat a fish, the result of these two events will affect the outcome of future events. The future events will not determine the past events. If you measure or detect something, your action of measuring or detecting something would not have caused the events prior to you measuring or detecting them but, it will change the events that happen in the future. Future events cannot change the events that already happened in the past. I think this is a basic law of the universe similar to the first and second laws of thermodynamics. You can always make up a story about how something happened like a small partial being at two places at the same time or the same photon taking two different paths at the same time but that is not necessarily how the observed event happened. In my view, the Copenhagen interpretation is realist to the extreme - perhaps even *more* so than the classical view. In the end, what it's saying is that an observation is a physical action, and the observer is part of the Universe and subject to the same laws as the system they try to observe. That was true already in classical physics; the new element introduced by quantum theory is that there is a limit to how *little* we can disturb an observed system and, what's more, the impossibility of measuring the effect of our action and retroactively taking it into account (in other words, we cannot even in theory reconstruct the state of the measured system *before* the measurement - we can only know what its state is *after* the measurement, which is what makes the measurement worthy of the name, btw).

This is like the question about whether or not a tree falling in the woods makes noise. Yes. How do you know? Well, because sound doesn't exist just because someone is there to hear it, and to make such a claim would require you demonstrating that there is a property to an observer that makes the thing miraculously appear as well as explain what happens to the sound should nobody be there to catch the sound waves from the fall as they go by.   For example, what if you have a person in a soundproof box that cannot hear anything outside and is only a dozen feet away from the tree. They can't hear it when it falls. All this means is the guy in the box separated from the tree won't know it fell until after the fact.   However if you add another person another dozen feet away from the tree, with the person in the soundproof box between the tree and the second person, then he will hear it fall and be aware of the action that happened.   But if you were to instead take the guy into the soundproof box and figure out a way to drop him in the same spot with such timing that the sound waves would pass his position just before he got there and reach the second person, you have the same effect.   The lack of an observer or ability to observe a physical phenomenon doesn't mean the phenomenon didn't happen, it just means the things necessary to observe it weren't there at the time.

So, simplified, you're basically saying that quantum systems gain "meaningful" existence from interacting with another quantum system. If you just started with that, you could've saved yourself 18 minutes.  Also, this, combined with the path of least energy suggests that any randomness is only in our observations and despite probability having been a real thing just "before" the first quantum systems that were part of this universe interacted, every interaction after that is super deterministic and inevitable. This is necessary for all the _other_ interactions to have the _other_ "random" outcomes. Kinda like how the universe isn't progressive slots, but you inevitably find "random" outcomes to approach an average, which isn't possible in a random undetermined universe because it should randomly go further from the average and entropy should randomly reverse and stuff, which can't happen because it's kept in check by interactions with surrounding quantum systems that make the outcomes set, not only based on their states and positions, but their entire past as well. And since it's impossible for any complex set of systems to have perfect knowledge of all other systems, we're still stuck with a relatively uncertain future for which we have to make choices based on information - this does not give us free will. Free will and choice have nothing to do with each other any more than a universe requires creation.

Ah, yes. The "universe". Something we named out of a desire for a subjective answer to the question of "what is it to be", only to proceed to question the definition and limitations of the word endlessly.  The universe is an enigma because its a word we used to try to encapsulate the vastness of  *everything* beyond the metaphysical, though we tend to treat it inconsistently as being metaphysical, physical, objective, subjective etc. Its another case of humans being limited by thinking about things and the words used to describe those things as being interchangeable as apposed to the word being a mere representation of said thing.  The problem is that we are asking questions that are ultimately either paradoxical in nature or theyre based more around the words we use to ask them than about the subject of the question.   When asking "Where did the universe come from" people often ask based on the fallacy that "the universe" refers to a thing (all things, everything, an objective thing), and therefore must have had a prior state as a "thing" cannot simply be created out of nothing and everything physical in our lives is so incredibly new on the scale of the universe and has come from somewhere else. This apposed to seeing the universe not as being made up of, but literally as being all of subjective reality, not just the "things" that we find in said reality.   We lived centuries under the influence of gravity, but it took an apple falling to the ground without being influenced for isaac newton to have the epiphany that theres an extraordinary force pulling us down at all times that might be explained by science. What we know, how we think and the questions we ask depend (far more than we like to admit) on our own personal perspectives and subjective interpretations over our existence than they do on objective observation or pure scientific exploration.  What if the universe is just the term we came up with to try to describe something that is beyond our own language? What if there is no 'The Universe', just universe endlessly in every direction. What if the universe was never created because it exists of nothing. What if space is just distance between matter and time is just distance between events. What if that relative relationship is what is responsible for what we interpret as being the universe. What if the universe is a metaphysical thing that we are trying too hard to rationalise.   Let me ask this; if atoms didnt make up anything larger, would we ever stop to ask if there was anything smaller? Maybe were just asking these big reaching questions about the origin of the universe because were hoping for the key to unlocking the universal theory of everything. Maybe its because were looking for something bigger than ourselves, a way to put our lives into perspective with the full scope of objective reality. Maybe its because we like thinking about questions that we dont know how to answer. But i think a part of it is that people dont even really understand what it is theyre asking or even why, its just something we do as humans. Stare at the stars, ask the big questions, yada yada.   I just think we should all do more to ask ourselves why we ask these questions in the first place, what the words were saying actually mean when we put them to questions, and what it is were actually hoping to figure out by doing so.

Iâ€™m not sure that â€œshut up and calculateâ€ makes any sense.    Steven Hawking claimed that physics does not try to explain reality, it only creates a system of math equations that predict observations.  But Hawkingâ€™s book A Short History of Time is chock-full of explanations of reality with nary an equation in sight.  I donâ€™t know if physics is even possible without explanations of reality because math principles by themselves are not enough to drive new insights.    For instance, when Kepler created equations to explain planet movement his data was just dots on a telescope that zigzagged across the sky over time.  If he was just doing math, he could have used any old function that gave the zigzag pattern.  But he was driven by the explanation that those dots were planets orbiting the sun.  That explanation for reality led him to his equations. Similarly, Einsteinâ€™s equations for general relativity were driven by his explanation that gravity is large objects curving space.  Once he came to this explanation, his equations could be derived from it.  I think Hawking and others have it backward.  You could argue that the job of physics is precisely to explain reality with metaphysical theories such as the big bang, quantum fields, and pilot wave theory.  The math that comes from those is used to confirm the metaphysical explanation.

Hello Matt! Here's a modification of the delayed-choice, as a thought experiment(without the quantum-erases). I call it the "Opposite Way experiment" We have the photon firing on the screen, while we are splitting after the split. The difference is, we make it so that second entangled pair, travels a really long distance, giving us the time to see the screen pattern, and effectively doing the OPPOSITE of what happens on the screen, by covering/uncovering the sensors. I.e, send the splitted particles to some mirrors on the moon(used in the past for distance/time measurements), and back where they will hit our sensors. This gives us around 2.51 seconds, to see the effect on the screen. (This can be modified if we can send to another, further, celestial body) 1) If we see solid lines, meaning we "Will" measure the particles in the next 2.51s, we will close the sensors and not read, which "Should" result in an interference pattern on the screen 2) If we see an interference pattern, meaning we "Will not" measure the particles in the next 2.51s, we will uncover the sensors, which "Should" result in 2 solid lines on the screen  What would happen in this case? The universe should either have a "default" state(like interference), or it could break down(not really, just a nice thought :P )

Afaik the computer just starts its 'print' command when needed, the difference is each dot will be in random order (so the universe or field knows the picture but the dots only create it over a larger statistical quantity). Pirsig's Metaphysics of Quality can give philosophical meaning to Wheeler's ideas. Pirsig left Quality undefined, but we may define it as the results of measurements taken by observers. To reinterpret Pirsig's example of exam papers through your lens: Pirsig asks why some exam papers are higher Quality than others, as measured by his students, and our answer is that the exam papers only have definite Quality when observed in a statistical ensemble; an exam paper is a quantum object.

I think Wheeler was on the right track, but missed an important point. Everything we sensate (via our eyes, skin, smell, taste, etc.,) results in a flow of electrons - via the nervous system - to the brain (from the eye, via stimulation of the retina; from the ears, via stimulation of the tympanic membrane, etc). Each electron is identical to the next - it's only specialised parts of the brain that decode the stimulus, and by doing so, assign an interpretation to it (this is an auditory stimulus; this is a visual stimulus, etc.) Forget theories of personality such as Freudian or Jungian - man is merely an information processor - all man is processing are electrons which gives rise to perception. To me Quantum Mechanics isnâ€™t about how the smallest particles behave. Itâ€™s about how isolated systems behave. So it could be said that it should be called Isolated Mechanics. That said, an â€œobserverâ€ suggests an intelligent being asking questions, also unnecessary confusement. Itâ€™s about energy or informational transfer between systems.  So to explain the reason behind the above statements, everything behaves like waves, like quantum mechanics describes. Every particle and even entire systems. Until information (energy) is transferred between 2 independent isolated systems, for example a photon and a detector. Then those 2 become 1 system (wavefunction) itself, as long as they are completely isolated from everything else (even thermally). This means that to the photon, the detector alongside the entire lab and everything else is also just 1 wavefunction.  So what does this mean for Schrodinger's cat? For the sake of completeness, this can only happen when â€œthe boxâ€ is completely radiationaly (so also thermally) isolated from everything else. Only then can the box be seen as a wavefunction. Within this wavefunction (the box) things can still exist and interact (the cat and the poison), reality can persist. The radiation that causes the poison to kill the cat only collapses the wavefunction of whatever is in the box to whatever is inside the box, not the wavefunction of the box itself, since the box is still isolated itâ€™s still a non collapsed wavefunction.   So what this means, from the outside looking at the box we can know nothing, it's in a superposition, since no information leaves the box. So, for as long as information inside the box has no influence on anything outside the box (the systems are isolated), the cat is dead and alive at the same time, only for everything outside the box and only because the outcome of its state can not be known and thus brings no difference to anything outside the box. Only when information leaves the box the 2 systems become 1. Whatever happened inside the box still happened but is only known to us when we look at it (when information has thansfered from the box to the outside).   So, no paradoxes were created here, at least in my always incomplete opinion.
Why is measurement seen as such a special thing? Even if you measure the distance traveled by a marble with a ruler next to it, the ruler will influence air currents, and exert gravity. It's just that those influences are too small to see. And when you're measuring something as small as a photon, such influences are bound to be much larger by comparison. No magical observer status required.

Wheeler is my hero, but he needs only 1 extra idea to complete the picture. The issue with consciousness is easily solved if you assume that the capacity for awareness is innate in the universe. There is nothing special about the human brain other than that which is physical, tangible. I like to put it this way: everything that happens is perceived, the only thing that matters is what is the nature of that perception? A pebble lacks a several billion neuron strong neural network, each thing that it perceives would be effectively meaningless without memory or cognitive processes to provide context. Wheeler is on the money, the past doesn't exist, only the present, but in this present moment you have access to all your memories, so you can ascribe meaning to what you perceive. A rock cannot do this, but it's purely because of the physical structure of the object, no magic or souls or what have you. A human could function just fine without being conscious/aware. Is a highly advanced robotic AI conscious? Think carefully before you answer because you yourself are essentially that robot. Unless you believe in souls or something the  you must admit we are just machines. So where does the conscious perception come in? It's just how the universe works. The occurrence and the perception of it are a single event. What matters is how intricate the occurrence is. The state of a human brain at any moment contains within it vast information from well outside its own local time and space. We remember things that have happened, and we collect data from far and wide through our senses, then crucially the neural net in our heads can correlate and organise all of this data into a single, present state of mind. So in essence, the only difference between a rock and a human brain is that one contains a lot more meaningful information than the other. Both are essentially "conscious", but the rock is totally braindead. Everything we associate with consciousness is just a brain function. Senses, memory, cognition, pain and pleasure, even dreams or drug induced states of consciousness. All of it is just the mechanics of the brain computer. If you strip all of that away what is left? I believe there is a profound difference between "non-awareness" and "awareness of nothing", if you follow me. To provide maybe some basis of a mathematical formalism for this, I think a useful tool might be to consider arbitrary regions of spacetime (a 4D volume) and ask questions like, "how much information about the outside system is encoded in the inside system?". A volume might be for example, a human brain over the course of 1 second, or a year, or a lifetime. It could be a pebble or even a planet or star over a million years. It could be the entire observable universe since the Big Bang. It could be an empty boring blob of deep space. Anything. This question is I think the best demonstration of what actually makes brains special and why. They are information collectors/concentrators. Whatever latent cosmic awareness happens to be perceiving that particular region of spacetime is going to have a far more colourful and meaningful experience than for the region of spacetime 1 foot to the left.

Until we look in the box, the cat may be both dead and alive simultaneously as best we know. However, once the box is opened and the cat's state is determined, we can work backward from that observation. If the cat is alive when the box is opened, it's logical to infer that the cat has remained alive all the way through -- given what we know about death and dying. And if the cat is dead when the box is opened, then various things can be measured to better determine the precise moment when the cat actually died -- rigor mortis level, how much oxygen remains in the box, etc.
If you put Schroedinger in a box with a cylinder of poisonous gas, he isn't "both dead or alive", he's one or the other. We just don't KNOW which until we open the box. A massive star a million light-years away it isn't both a star and a supernova until we see it blow, it's one or the other. Schroedinger needed a proper job :P

I have always said that one of the most important questions ever asked was "If a tree falls in the woods and no one is around to hear it, does it make a sound?". I have been grappling with this idea for many years so its nice know I was not alone in this thought and that this idea has proper name, the participatory universe.  TL;DR Time only exists because of observers.  Sorry this is my brain vomit from years of thought...  If you are confused as to why I think the above question is the most important, its the idea that "no one is around to hear it" implies that the event is not measured, ever, for all time. If an event is not measured, did it even happen? Remember you answer! You can't say you observe it's effects on other things, because that an indirect observation of the event, no one and no thing measures this event.  You might think this trivial but wait, this get interesting and paradoxical! A thought experiment will make this idea concrete. Say their is by chance a black hole swarm that is absolutely massive surrounding a region of space with only a Newtons cradle clacking back and forth. The swarm is creating an enclosing sphere that has gaps so outside observers could theoretically get information of the cradle clacking away. The swarm then closes uniformly and perfectly, filling the gaps and making an extremely massive event horizon enclosing the central region. Now the Newtons cradle is suspended in an ever enclosing event horizon from all direction as the singularities continue their fall to the center. At this point the Newtons cradle is behind the event horizon, so I ask you as an outside observer of the swarm/event horizon, is the Newtons cradle still clacking? For all you know the central region fell out instantly when the connection to the rest of the universe was severed, or the inner event horizon instantly closed and destroyed the cradle, or something else entirely. You can't observe the event, ever...so the question now is "Does the universe observe itself". If it does, then the cradle continues to clack before its inevitable doom, even thought you can't prove it. If the universe does not observe itself, then something very *weird* must be true.  The above idea is a precursor to a bigger thought I have been grappling with that is very philosophical and scientific; this is my argument for the universe NOT being able to observe itself..."Does time exist with no observer?" I argue the universe should have a clock speed of "infinity fast", but as soon as an observer is created in the universe the clock is set to the speed of the observer. Why? What stops the speed of time faster than what it is? Is it not true that the universe could run an 2x speed (like a 2x speed YouTube video) with all laws of reality the same and nothing would be different? Why is it that the universe seems to tick at a rate we can comprehend. "Forces and constants and xyz maintain that the universe ticks a non infinite speed and constrain causality to be what we observe it today" you might say.........so do forces and constants and the xyz's exist when their is no observer, ever? Well I think not, remember you answer from the question above! Even if forces and constants do exist without observers time could run at infinite speed and it would not make a difference to reality anyway!  These are my shower thoughts, observers make time tick at non infinite speeds...and what does this imply.....that *an observer is more than just some particles in the universe...the observer is something very, very special*.
I hope we never find out.

Here's an idea to look into: assume a base unit of spacetime -- say a "stixel" one planck-length in diameter and a unit of planck-time in duration -- but this has equal measurement in every part of the universe at a specific instant, whether matter/energy is present or not; also, while acknowledging that the ratio of this unit and the size of the universe would be infinite, assume that for any two points in time (X & Y), there is a finite ratio between the stixel at X & the stixel at Y. What if there is an observation that narrows the wave function down, but doesn't collapse it completely?  For example, imagine the double slit experiment, but this time we have 4 slits. When there are no detectors on the slits, we get an interference pattern. When we have a detector on each slit, we see the outlines of the 4 slits. But what if we have only two detectors, each one only telling us whether a particle went though one of a pair of slits or not. This way, the detectors only narrow it down to two slits instead of 1. In this case, would we see an interference pattern created by the two slits, or would we see the outline of the two slits in the detection? Why?  Also, in the case of us "creating our reality" by asking questions, would the resolution of our observations define the detail with which reality is able to definite itself? For example, what if bob asked marry "what color is the cat" and marry said "not yellow". In this case, this has only narrowed down the possibility space a little bit, but it is still continuous in the color dimension. You could say that this is a binary question in disguise, and it is basically the same as asking "is the cat yellow", but I think it would be more accurate to say that we are asking marry to choose a color out of, say, ROY G. BIV than a binary question. If she had answered "Green", our possibility space would be narrowed down more, but we still do not have only one possibility for the color, as there are many different shades of green. It seems to me, that you could never fully "collapse" the possibility space by asking any sort of question, especially binary questions.  I think this principle applies for things like telescopes too. In an ordinary camera, we have 2^24 color possibilities for each pixel (256 possibilities for Red, Green, and Blue). When you take a picture of a star or something, you narrow collapse the wave function of that star to one possibility out of all the possible values for each pixel on your camera. This, however, does not eliminate every possibility for the nature of that star, as there are many more shades of colors in the visual spectrum than 2^24, and we only have a limited number of pixels. Despite gaining a lot of information, there is still a continuum of possibilities for this star to exist in. It seems to me that we have not full collapsed the system's wave function, if there is such a possibility.  So, if a wave function collapses, does it always collapse to a single point of infinite probability, or is it possible to partially collapse wave functions? If they always "fully collapse" how do we know? If not, doesn't this basically mean that there's no base, objective reality that is possible to verify?

I don't know why physicists so stubbornly refuse to refer to de Broglie / pilot-wave theory.  It got a passing mention here, but when thinking in these terms, so many of the "paradoxes" of quantum mechanics just go away.  Like the delayed choice experiment, so mind-bending in the Coopenhagen interpretation, is straightforward in the pilot-wave interpretation.  The second beam-splitter lets the wavefunction interfere with itself, and the particle follows the wavefunction.  It simply can't go where the wavefunction cancels itself out.  No delayed choice is being made, the observer has no effect, and all along everything is just as real and physical as if the photons were billiard balls.  Maybe it's just me but whenever I'm puzzled by a quantum effect I consider it in terms of pilot-wave theory and it starts to make sense. The biggest mistake people make when describing and/or interpreting "observed" is (incorrectly) thinking that "observe" = "look at with your eyes." This has led to some silly conclusions that have made their way into pop culture. The _correct_ definition "observe" is measuring. How do we measure things so small? We need detectors of some sort. How do these detectors work? They use light. The energy of the light we use to measure these things actually affects the system we're trying to measure.

Something I find people have difficulty with is that just as there are many possible futures, there are many pasts, all of which are completely consistent with all available information available in the present, all of which are relevant. Quantum theory speaks to this, however the concept of multiple pasts of equal validity is not well known or understood by the public. The present is a product, a construction of all possible pasts. The key is all possible pasts, all of which must be entirely consistent with the present. I suspect, although I am not fully convinced, that the present can be described as an interference pattern of all possible pasts upon the current physical universe combined. A combination of all possible assemblages of data that the Universe is composed of.  Time, and disorder as well, have a symmetry about the present. Physical laws are time symmetrical. The implications are both profound and fascinating. No, the friend of the SchrÃ¶dingerâ€™s cat physicist does not â€œwait onâ€ the result of the first unethical dead-cat experiment. As an English-speaking physicist who didnâ€™t learn the language from internet rednecks posting vlogs from their pickup trucks, he waits FOR the result.  Not that he should wait for the result, since there is no actual experiment in which the results of life-or-death actions depend on quantum entanglement. The danger one faces when devising thought experiments is that they may correspond to no thinkable actual experiment. Not only canâ€™t we conduct a cat-boxed experiment of this sort in actual labs, even if we were (evilly) inclined to do it, itâ€™s hard to imagine circumstances in which entanglement could play such a part as the thought experiment suggests.  This is bad both for the thought experiment and for the interpretation it was intended to counter. The idea that nothing exists as such until we measure it - rather than nothing exists as we measure it until we measure it, which would be a more prudent formulation - has about the same status as the claim that no event occurred unless we have pictures. Itâ€™s good fodder for vlogs, but doesnâ€™t carry much philosophical weight.  A more substantial physical idealism can be formulated, though the Bishop Berkeley of quantum idealism has yet to appear. Still, people like it when physicists attempt to do ontology. How else can we explain the popularity of the brain-dead anthropical principle among physicists and physics fanciers? Decades have passed since we heard we should shut up and calculate, and we have a buffet of half-cooked interpretations of quantum mechanics as an ontology starting point - which makes the persistence of the Bohr weak-tea version all the more curious. The examples we review in the video, however - including JAWâ€™s - fail to make a much better case.  Bonus note: the double beam-split example tells us a very simple thing: observing a process changes it ever more the smaller the observed process is, with particle-level observations making definitive changes to them. At the level of vloggers and viewers, our observation is not immaterial, but it is not enough to define the vlogger or the vlog. Were we detecting single photons, the impact of our observation is enough to change the manner of transit, simply because observation requires interaction that is material to photons.  The doubled beam splitting tells us, yes, there is superposition, but no, our presence is not creating anything, our minds and choices do not create anything by their mere observational presence or interrogation. Itâ€™s the mechanisms of the observation and inquiry that have physical effects, in principle and practice. Our observation does not play a part in the â€œrealityâ€ of the situation except so far as we intervene to make things behave in certain ways. That is, this experiment tells us the opposite of what people insisting on quantum weirdness want it to tell us. The photon beam happily travels both paths until something funnels it this way or that or both - which way the photons go may or may not surprise us, but the change as change is entirely expected.
The universe is not rendered when it isn't being observed, in the same sense a video game doesn't render the parts of the map a player isn't looking at. These parts still "evolve" according to the rules of the game, very much like the universe evolve according to Schrodinger's equation.
