Here's a crazy idea: Dark energy isn't needed to explain the universe. The further you look in the universe, the quicker stuff seems to move away from us. But that's logical when you think of the big bang as an 'explosion' where everything moved away from each other very quickly (near light speed). In time stuff will move slower from each other due to gravity. So the longer you wait, the slower it moves. But when you look far away, you're looking back in time when everything still moves fast. So no wonder, the further away you look, the quicker things move away.  Also: Everything is moving away from each other in the universe. So in the past, stuff was closer together, which is why the big bang was postulated. But when you look really far away, you look back in time when everything was still close together. So when you take two points far away 90 degrees from each other, from our perspective those points are far far more light-years apart from each other than what they actually are. That means stuff should look smeared out over space at that distance. Galaxies should look bigger and fainter/red-shifted.

The popular understanding of the Many-Worlds interpretation says that each reaction causes new universes to form, but what if it's the opposite? What if each reaction collapses the universal wave-form to only worlds that each have the exact same starting conditions? That would mean that every universe is exactly like ours, up to each individual moment of time, but all those where different outcomes transpired cease to exist the moment they differentiate?   That would allow for a universe that was both probabilistic, and deterministic, at the same time, and we as conscious beings would also still retain our free will. It would also bring about the lack of evidence for a multiverse... since all universes would be identical to our own.

So. I keep seeing these videos about how quantum particles become localized when measured. But how about the other way? How does thise quantum superpositioned particles get produced or converted to superposition? We never really hear about that. Cause, if they get localized when measured by any observant, aka interacts with something localized, then why have we not run out of these superpositioned particles yet?
I really do think I have a model, somewhat similar to this, that could explain all of these seeming discrepancies and paradoxes...  But it goes a step further and doesn't require participation from an "observer" (although that observer is still a participating component), or that the universe be "made of information" (although it certainly contains it, and the relationship between information and reality are inseparable)  It just requires that we go all in on QFT and embrace the concept that the only thing that exists is the now... (although now for me and now for you do not need to be the same unless we are causally linked and sharing the same reference frame)..  Basically all of reality, you, me, the sun, the andromeda galaxy, and everything in between are really just expressions of the magnitude of the amount of potential and kinetic energy contained within and distributed throughout the quantum fields.... sort of points of high pressure constrained by the degrees of freedom in the fields, the fundamental properties of the fields, and the interactions between the various regions of high and low pressure both in between and within these fields. Collapse is nothing more than when a pressure wave in one field, exchanges enough virtual particles with another to collapse the entire pressure wave out of the field in which it's propagating into the field where that potential energy manifests as a point particle in that field, kind of like punching a hole in a high pressure canister, the point where all the high pressure air is rushing out of is your particle.  The reason this can fix so much of the quantum weirdness that we see in these thought experiments is that it allows for systems to evolve locally, but then have an ability for "correction" should a "conflict" arise between regions of spacetime when the information (and energy) from the disparate regions come together and interact, allowing  "spooky action at a distance" to work without breaking locality. It also eliminates the paradoxes like Schrodinger's cat without requiring many worlds or extra spatial dimensions  to try and sum it up as simply as I can, it's a bit like last thursdayism in the sense that it's built on the premise that all of the information we have about the past can only be known as the current expression of the sum of all the past information that gave rise to the current state that we experience as "now". Explained in terms of fields, if we imagine the entire universe as a grid of fields, and that the properties of any one point in the universe are determined by the values of the degrees of freedom in each grid box, then the values of the current state depend on prior values and the exchange of values with surrounding boxes.  I set things up this way because it illustrates that all you really know about the past is the information available to you when you take a measurement of those values, and knowledge of the past is extrapolated from those current measurements. So there is no real way of being certain whether the information actually represents the past, or if the universe just popped into existence the way it looks last thursday  To be clear, I'm not advocating for last thursdayism, or suggesting that the universe is any younger than it appears. I am just setting up the premise that underlies the proposed mechanism.  Anyway, with all that out of the way... what if the reality that we experience is the sum of colliding probability waves that actually carry potential energy through the quantum fields, and the sum of two waves may create an alternate version of the current experience than what would be experienced given the path of just a single wave.  Put more plainly, and relevant to the locality issue, what if when measured, each of the entangled particles give an independent measurement that is is non correlated, at first. But when the two seperate probability waves that were triggered during measurement of the particles eventually collide, they interfere and create a single new probability wave that is consistent with the original properties imparted by the entanglement.  Even more plainly. when two entangled particles, one on earth and one on the moon, are measured, you get whatever you measure, and it's random, but when the information about the measurements is exchanged, the field states re-align to account for the sum of the probabilities, and what we observe is information that agrees with the properties of entangled particles.  So when you measure one, you don't actually know what the current state of the other particle is, but instead you know what the INFORMATION about the state WILL BE when you receive that information... or at least you think you do, because your current reality may be rewritten once the information is exchanged, and your current reality may, in a way, cease to exist.  I hope that made sense... hard concept to communicate with words.. looks better when expressed as summation of probability equations.. lol.. I personally really like this hypothesis as it is extremely consistent, both internally, and with experimental evidence, and it leaves no paradoxes that I am aware of...  It really seems to be the best fit for all of the seeming paradoxes that arise from quantum entanglement, Schrodinger's cat and experiments like the delayed choice quantum eraser experiment.  Or at least, it seems a plausible fit to me.  I just wish I had actually finished my degree so I could actually do something with the idea.. But hey.. this is quantum mechanics...  So if history is anything to go by, I'm probably completely out to lunch.. lol Cheers!

Here's an idea: The past and the future(s) are the same thing. Imagine an arrow with the potential to point in all directions in time, different futures and pasts. Almost all directions lead to an higher entropy. There's only some that have lower entropy. Now optimize for the direction of the lowest entropy. Do a step in the direction of that arrow and repeat that process. It lines out the path to a state where the entropy is 0. There is only one shortest path. But there are many where the entropy gets higher. Now the idea. I state that all possible paths exist. The question is why the universe's entropy was so low at the beginning of time. But was it actually? When you are thinking of the past you are just imagining an optimal path of many possible paths through time where the entropy is lower, and call that 'the past'. You can imagine any different path where entropy gets higher. All are equally real, so the one leading to entropy 0 as well. Why was entropy so low in the past? Because we define it to be so.

I think some quotes from Schrodinger, Heisenberg and Freeman Dyson are worth reading in the paper and Smolin and Verde make sense. The general idea is that wave functions only exist in the future. The passage of time is bound up with wave function collapse. SchrÃ¶dingerâ€™s cat is not in a superposition of alive and dead in the past or the present, it is actually alive or dead. Considering that so many crazy paradoxes *exist only in the Copenhagen interpretation* (e.g. measurements don't affect reality in the many-worlds interpretation), maybe the problem is not the universe but this specific interpretation?  BTW, the many-world interpretation should be the default, since it assumes nothing except the well-tested theories of quantum mechanics.  Instead the Copenhagen "interpretation" is really a separate and unverified theory, since it implicitly assumes that the brains of scientists work exclusively on classical physics and can never be affected by the quantum mechanics laws that govern the rest of the universe. Or the light really travels both paths in the Delayed Choice experiment, because it's an unquantized wave and not a particle.  It would appear quantized if it's also "self-entangled," by which I mean absorption of part of the wave causes absorption of the entire wave, even if some of the wave was far away (outside the past lightcone) from the absorption location a moment before the absorption.  Since quantum mechanics already includes entanglement phenomena, "self-entanglement" of a wave doesn't seem implausible, and perhaps this interpretation is simpler in the Occam's Razor sense.

Ive had this hypothesis floating around my head for a bit, and I wanted to get some input on it. This hypothesis is closely related to the Big Bounce hypothesis, but it varies in some aspects. Hypothesis: When the universe becomes completely uniform time will "reverse", eventually turning our universe into one with white holes, is dominated by antimatter, and is crunching in on itself. This could explain why we dont see white holes or much antimatter at all in our universe without having to resort to extremely complex equations that have yet to precisely model our universe. As scientists have already hashed out, reversing time doesnt make a difference in terms of physics. Reversing time doesnt break our equations, our equations actually work exactly the same if time is reversed. The only thing it does is change matter to antimatter and black holes into white holes. So whats stopping a fragile, uniform universe from not being uniform anymore? Whats stopping time from reversing when entropy has nowhere else to go? And when time is reversed and the universe eventually crunches in on itself, whats stopping the extreme environment of the subatomic universe from rebounding and time going forward again? This hypothesis differs from the big bounce hypothesis by introducing the concept that time can reverse when entropy ceases. Its not just "our universe will expand to a certain point then crunch back in on itself". This hypothesis argues that the universe will crunch back in on itself by time reversing when entropy can no longer make things any more uniform. Whats more interesting is that the previously reversing universe that turned into our big bang could have left an imprint on the CMB. Something that multiverse theory proponents say could only happen from a universe outside of our own. Well, now they have competition if we ever do find an imprint on the CMB.

Iâ€˜m a mathematician, and I struggle hard to get a straight answer from physicists on the following questions:  1. given an observation, how do you calculate forward or backward the wave function in time? Is that even meaningful?  2. Is it fair to compare quantum mechanics to a hidden Markov model if you replace the Markov transition operator with some unitary operator and the emission operator with the absolute square of a projection?  If 2. is the case, then in my mind, the mystery would basically go away. If you think about it, a hidden Markov model is (just like a Markov chain) just an encoding of the statistics of the observable marginal process. Often, the hidden process is entirely made up and just serves to reduce the complexity of the data to something that we can deal with mathematically.  The same might be true for the wave function: even though you kinda need it to effectively calculate the statistics of quantum measurements, it may be entirely made up. Note that one doesnâ€™t have to treat mathematics as â€just a languageâ€œ or say that physical models are always â€just models [that donâ€™t say anything about fundamental reality]â€œ. One only has to know when to make this distinction.  If my speculation is correct, we might learn something about fundamental reality by analyzing the class of observable processes that quantum mechanics encodes. However, I wouldnâ€™t expect efficient predictive algorithms from that.

I'm not a physicist, but this video does exemplify why physicists need to stay out of philosophy. They are trying to apply laws in metaphysics and epistemology to particles, and I don't think they translate. Do this experiment with a real object we can iterate with - like a rock or a ball instead of a particle - and you will actually have my attention.
This is reminiscent of "lazy execution" in computer science, where a piece of state is generated on-demand instead of being computed every time there's a change to the system. For example, a rectangle defined by two points on a 2D plane, where the object is manipulated by moving the points around, but every 100 manipulations or so the program needs to query the width of the rectangle. The object can store a "dirty" flag, which indicates that its remembered width is out-of-date. Any time there's a change to a point, it sets the dirty flag to true, then when the width is queried it checks the flag. If the flag is false then it returns the previously calculated value, but if the flag is true then it calculates the width, remembers it, and sets the flag to false, then returns the result of the query. This makes the query itself more expensive, but because the query only happens 1% as often as the manipulations, it results in an overall savings, since the width isn't constantly being recalculated when there's no use for it.

Whoa whoa whoa... "Information implies a thing to know that information."??? It does no such a thing.  You're playing a semantic game here and a really bad one. Information is "data with meaning" and that's a human construct. Another way to look at it is "Information is APPLIED data." If I collect the temperatures of everyone on earth at exactly 1pm GMT, that's data. If I choose to ask "put them into 1 degree buckets" - I've changed the data into information. But the act of choosing doesn't require a human consciousness. If two balls strike each other, the data of their trajectories and momenta is measured at the moment of collision by the balls AS A SYSTEM providing information to determine the new outcome trajectories and momenta.   Wheeler is correct: it's the interactions that matter and are the measurements (or at least define them). When two electrons interact in such a way that their spin orientations MUST have a definite value, that causes a measurement to happen and the electrons to gain a state. The interaction IS the measurement. But to take it further, there is NO difference between the probe and the probed. Every "measurement" affects both parties, although not necessarily the same way or to the same degree.   Our measurements *look* conscious because WE are conscious and we choose the nature of the measurement. That's like going back to my temperature example and after measuring and binning handing the numbers to someone unaware of their origin and their looking at the results and saying "This is weird... none of the temperatures are below 34C or above 42C... and most of them are at at 37C. That's not natural - some mysterious is happening."  Wheeler's model (lord help me, I'm defending Wheeler) is not vague or wishy washy at all. In fact it's very concrete and simple. Having the universe be transactional and self-generating is vague and wishy washy.

One possible solution to the 'observer problem' is panpsychism. Specifically, panprotoexperientialism, which basically asserts that there is a "proto-conscious" field that permeates all of reality, and would act as a universal, omnipresent 'observer' at all points in space.  Proto-consciousness therefore causes wave function collapse. It's a super weak field though, weaker even than gravity, and would only have an effect on quantum events where an observer made a difference.

I have to ask..  Define the difference between measurement and interaction.  The universe interacts with itself all the time, collapsing wave functions.  "Measurement" does not make it any more real.  If A interacts with B or B interacts with A, depending on scale collapsing wave functions without us even measuring it. Also, I want to point out that the "objective reality exists regardless of our subjective experience of it" is, by definition, a non-scientific claim. This is for logical and philosophical reasons, not because I suspect that the universe isn't actually real.  Here's the rub, in a nutshell: Scientists love to claim that science is completely empirically based. Empiricism means based on our perception - our senses. This, quite literally, means based on our subjective experience. The idea is that our sense experience is the most foundational knowledge we have of anything at all, and therefore the basis, the very foundation, of knowing itself. You know nothing, if not first through sensing something through one of your senses. Thus, empiricism. The world is sensed.  However, the twist in thinking that way, is that if you assume the basis of reality is sensory, then you are also assuming that it is subjective, because your sensory experiences are subjective.  In other words, you've simply given in to the solipsism idea.  The claim that reality is independent of any awareness of it is unfalsifiable by definition. If you aren't aware of it, then you can't make any claim about it whatsoever, and if you are aware of it, then you cannot make a claim independent of awareness.  Therefore, logically, reality cannot be claimed to be independent of awareness. I mean, it might be. It makes sense that it could be. But, according to the rules of falsifiability, it's an unscientific question, because there's no way you could ever device an experiment that shows that reality is completely independent of your awareness.  Solipsism is, in fact, irrefutable, at least on a logical, scientific basis. The world really could be just a delusion of yours, and maybe none of it has any existence apart from your mind. That may or may not be true, but you have no way to prove that it's true - and more importantly, there is no empirical way to prove it false.
